Okay, so what we want to do, we understood the problem of image classification. And we saw our challenges. What you want to do at the end of the day, you want a function like this. The function is, takes as input the image, and if the function has to do something, then it has to output the class table. The problem is, because of all the challenges that we saw, unlike sorting numbers for which we have a concrete algorithm, we, until today, do not have an obvious way to hard-code an algorithm to recognize things like cats and fish and so on. We have tried for decades in the past to come up with some algorithm using how humans do it and so many ways to somehow find out algorithms that you can write with your class table. We don't have any obvious way to do that. Because we don't have any obvious way to do that, people have tried all this: finding corners, finding edges, they all have different different challenges. Because there's no obvious way to do that, that is why we need machine-learning based method. So what we're going to do in the machine-learning data-driven approach is we're going to collect a dataset of images and labels. So you can say the categories that you care about are airplane, automobile, bird, cat and deer. So what you're going to do is for each of those categories, you're going to collect a lot of examples of airplanes, examples of automobile, examples of bird, examples of cat and so on. That's step number 1. And step number 2: you're going to use machine-learning to use this data to automatically figure out what the function should be that maps the images to the correct categories. You are not going to write that function on your own but you are going to try and learn a system which learns this function on its own from the data. This is also a supervised learning problem: X is the image and the labels are these categories, whether it's airplane or automobiles. It's a supervised learning problem. You're going to train up machine learning system to build this, learn this function and as I mentioned earlier, the goal of learning this function is to then when you have a new image, the predict function that you have, you and the trained model, you want your system to perform well on new images. That is the goal. The goal in learning from the training example is to make predictions on new unseen things. This is the machine learning model. Learning outcomes: after learning this course, you should be able to analyze specific requirements to derive functional and non-functional things. When I looked at a lot of designs and architectures for use cases from different sources (companies, government, etc.), I feel that most of them are the professional or complete steps of the assessment system design. The first one is functional requirements analysis. I feel that a lot of students understand functional requirements analysis, because that's what we learn in software engineering. We need to learn the first step: I need to understand what we have to achieve, what's the functionality required by our clients. The scale of applications, due to distributed computing, due to so many new technologies, we have to analyze non-functional requirements. For example, scalability, performance. When we talk about performance, we often talk about latency. So functional requirements and non-functional requirements are the first two steps when designing architecture for a given use case. And then we are willing to apply consistency models, replication strategies, and distributed conceptions patterns to design correct and available distributed systems. And then we are going to select and config appropriate AWS services for given use cases and justify those choices within the well-architected framework. You know what you need to use just like when you build a house you need to know what tools you're gonna use. Those tools correspond to the AWS services we're gonna learn. The same thing, when we build our architecture for an application we need to understand the features of our services and then we know which ones are going to be used and design caching hierarchies, load balancing and the auto scaling policies to achieve the scalability and their variable effort. This is very, very important. Building scalable applications is almost a requirement of every single application nowadays when I see the requirements from clients. Even though when you build a relatively quite small application for a start-up company the scale is very small but in the requirement or the considerations of application design they mention we need to consider scalability because we need to scale for the future right? You cannot build a system that only works for now. You have to build a system or application that can be scaled. You cannot redesign your application because it scales right? So you need to consider that. We design applications from the beginning and architecture asynchronous event driven systems using message cubes and with explicitly defined deliberate guarantees. We don't like tightly coupled systems. We want loosely coupled systems. In this chapter, we are going to learn the introduction to cloud architecting. It will give you the overall concept and frameworks of what we will be following while we architect for a given business. For the objectives of cloud architecting, not for this chapter:
- Overall, if you learn cloud architecting, what you are trying to achieve is to apply architectural principles and the best practices to make architectural decisions similar to system design.
- Use appropriate AWS services. Appropriate means trade-offs and features to make infrastructure scalable, reliable, and readily available.
- Choose AWS managed services. Can you give me an example of managed services? Give me a service that's not managed by AWS. EC2 is a representative service that's not managed by AWS.
- To get greater flexibility and resiliency in an infrastructure, increase performance and reduce the cost of a cloud infrastructure built on AWS, use AWS services and features to secure user applications and data access.
- Apply best practices from the AWS web architecture framework to improve architectures that use AWS solutions.
This is a very high-level, general objective where you learn how to architect it.
Now let's look at the web architecture framework. I used to show this to students before. Cloud Architecting: that link will direct you to this page. It's the official AWS Web Architecture Framework documentation over here.
There are six pillars you can see on the left side in the navigation bar. The pillars of the framework include:
- Operational excellence
- Security
- Reliability
- Performance efficiency
- Cost optimization
- Sustainability
The newest one, relatively newest one, is sustainability. The last one, previously we only had five pillars, but even sustainability was added at least three years ago.
Open one, and you can see the design principles and definition, best practices, and it contains organization, prepare, operate, evolve, resources. You can see how much it contains, right? The well-architected framework contains a lot of things.
If you are going to write the exam for the SAA certification (SAA Solution Architecture Associate), you have to understand all the stuff in this framework. If you want to design a good architecture for a given use case, you have to follow this framework. When we learn services, actually, we learn a lot of stuff inside this framework. This is a generalized and well-formatted documentation for us to learn.

So this is an empty pattern so the best practice of scalability is that users rarely experience a service interruption because new server is ready before capacity is reached. So when you reach 80 percent of the CPU load of all the servers a new server is being provisioned so after this new server is being provisioned your servers could reach 100 percent of those average but the load will be distributed to this new server right so you won't experience that interruption that's how most of the modern systems are running nowadays uh with systems are using cloud they almost all of them are using auto-scaling second, automating your environment actually the first case already tells you you need to automate things you need to automate the scaling not manually scaling right but it's a different thing for a different way to automate your environment. Choosing the right database solution. Right database solution depends on a lot of factors, like read and write needs. Most databases have way more read-on-read operations than write operations, right? So total storage requirements. Is your storage very, very big? And total object size and nature of access to those objects. Durability requirements and latency requirements. Maximum concurrent users to support. Nature of queries and required strength of integrity controls So of course today we are not analyzing all these. But we need to understand that when we choose the right database solution we need to consider these. And also, especially for databases we need to avoid single points of failure. So the single point of failure in a database is like this: when this database is gone, the instance is gone you can not access through this. So you can't go to the database anymore from your application tier. So that's why we learned that we have a secondary or standby database multi-AZ. Multi-AZ standby or standard. You have a primary database and you have a replication of that database as standby or secondary so when the primary database is gone or crashes, the secondary database can be failed over to take the load from your users. Right? The request from users. It's usually rack-mounted and this tall or this tall. So that server has a big portion of that server is for drives, we have a lot of hard drives. Those drives are called disk arrays. So the server manage the disk arrays using software. So like this we have a lot of slots. These slots are the disk disks you can insert and push in and there's a latch on each hard drive. The hard drive is different from our normal hard drive. There's a latch on that you can easily push a button and then remove the hard drive and push a new one in storage. And the software managing this disk array will have to interact with the disk array, interact with the physical part to make the swap of the hard drives safe and won't impact other things. So in most cases, this kind of storage arrays, when you swap out and insert a new hard drive, it's called half-swappable, you won't impact the running system. There's no interruption, no downtime. And usually in this kind of system, you can tolerate one or two drives that can be broken at the same time still that won't impact our performance, but we need to replace them as soon as possible. It's replicated. Okay? So your data actually is divided, every part is saved at different data nodes so you have at least you have three copies of your data and they are at different data nodes. Okay now let's look at big data. Big data used to have five bits but now it has six bits. Did you hear these bits people? Yeah I believe so. So the first one is volume. The size of the data is huge. From terabytes to exabytes, in between is terabytes. Velocity is a speed of arrival streaming like from a lot of IOT devices, the data is very, very quick. Shapes, they are structured data taxed to binary data or all different kind of data and veracity like the data can be clean can be messy. We do not know what they are the trustworthiness of the data is Unknown. And the value of the data, we don't know the value of the data. It could be very, very valuable. It could be garbage. Here the scenario is a flash sale inventory system. The scenario is that an e-commerce platform runs a one-hour flash sale. A product has 500 units available. They only have 500 left, so thousands of users attempt to purchase simultaneously. The system must never oversell and must also remain responsive under peak load. The requirements are:
- Purchase must be atomic, never sell more than 500 units.
- Order history must be queryable by user and by date.
- User sessions and cart data must be fast.
- Systems must stay available even if one region has network issues.
- Availability is very important.
- It should handle 10,000 concurrent purchase attempts at peak.
- Every customer can only buy one unit.
These are the requirements. Now we need to design this application. The major design is about databases. We don't need to design all the API, the microservices, et cetera, but you can show them in the architecture. The major design is about databases. In this chapter, we are going to learn the introduction to cloud architecting. It will give you the overall concept and frameworks of what we will be following while we architect for a given business. For the objectives of cloud architecting, not for this chapter:
- Overall, if you learn cloud architecting, what you are trying to achieve is to apply architectural principles and the best practices to make architectural decisions similar to system design.
- Use appropriate AWS services. Appropriate means trade-offs and features to make infrastructure scalable, reliable, and readily available.
- Choose AWS managed services. Can you give me an example of managed services? Give me a service that's not managed by AWS. EC2 is a representative service that's not managed by AWS.
- To get greater flexibility and resiliency in an infrastructure, increase performance and reduce the cost of a cloud infrastructure built on AWS, use AWS services and features to secure user applications and data access.
- Apply best practices from the AWS web architecture framework to improve architectures that use AWS solutions.
This is a very high-level, general objective where you learn how to architect it.
Now let's look at the web architecture framework. I used to show this to students before. Cloud Architecting: that link will direct you to this page. It's the official AWS Web Architecture Framework documentation over here.
There are six pillars you can see on the left side in the navigation bar. The pillars of the framework include:
- Operational excellence
- Security
- Reliability
- Performance efficiency
- Cost optimization
- Sustainability
The newest one, relatively newest one, is sustainability. The last one, previously we only had five pillars, but even sustainability was added at least three years ago.
Open one, and you can see the design principles and definition, best practices, and it contains organization, prepare, operate, evolve, resources. You can see how much it contains, right? The well-architected framework contains a lot of things.
If you are going to write the exam for the SAA certification (SAA Solution Architecture Associate), you have to understand all the stuff in this framework. If you want to design a good architecture for a given use case, you have to follow this framework. When we learn services, actually, we learn a lot of stuff inside this framework. This is a generalized and well-formatted documentation for us to learn.

For example if you have your cloud watch is monitoring your scaling if all the instances are running at 80% of the load average CPU load average that's a threshold that you set up so the cloud watch is going to detect that and then when that threshold is reached we automatically scale our machine. So this is an empty pattern so the best practice of scalability is that users rarely experience a service interruption because new server is ready before capacity is reached. So when you reach 80 percent of the CPU load of all the servers a new server is being provisioned so after this new server is being provisioned your servers could reach 100 percent of those average but the load will be distributed to this new server right so you won't experience that interruption that's how most of the modern systems are running nowadays uh with systems are using cloud they almost all of them are using auto-scaling second, automating your environment actually the first case already tells you you need to automate things you need to automate the scaling not manually scaling right but it's a different thing for a different way to automate your environment. Choosing the right database solution. Right database solution depends on a lot of factors, like read and write needs. Most databases have way more read-on-read operations than write operations, right? So total storage requirements. Is your storage very, very big? And total object size and nature of access to those objects. Durability requirements and latency requirements. Maximum concurrent users to support. Nature of queries and required strength of integrity controls So of course today we are not analyzing all these. But we need to understand that when we choose the right database solution we need to consider these. And also, especially for databases we need to avoid single points of failure. So the single point of failure in a database is like this: when this database is gone, the instance is gone you can not access through this. So you can't go to the database anymore from your application tier. So that's why we learned that we have a secondary or standby database multi-AZ. Multi-AZ standby or standard. You have a primary database and you have a replication of that database as standby or secondary so when the primary database is gone or crashes, the secondary database can be failed over to take the load from your users. Right? The request from users. - If your application would be general, of course you are choosing like Gemini, GPT; those are limited models, right, but if your application is quite domain-specific, you will need to do some research. There could be very good models for your domain and training data.
- Evaluate whether the LLM has been trained on data that matches the domain or context of the project.

Okay, model size and complexity: a model with tens of billions of parameters provides higher quality output that requires more compute resources. That's why I said we want to do better time by SLM. Not only do we save space and compute power, but it is also quicker. Adapting and tuning, determining if the children's LLM can be effectively contextualized with prompts or fine-tuning.

When you improve the performance of an LLM using prompts compared to fine-tuning the LLM, which way do you have to change the model? Fine-tuning. Fine-tuning, yes. Prompt engineering. Prompt engineers bridge the gap between end users and add-ons. When we say prompt engineering here, we don't mean how we are going to improve the prompt on the web interface of JTPT. It's not like that. It's in between how developers improve the prompt. Prompt engineering is not for end users, actually; it's for developers. It bridges the gap between them, right? We are not having users. Of course, if you can write a good prompt, our prompt engineers' work will be easier. These engineers experiment with different types of equals to build a prompt library that application developers can reuse. You see, developers can reuse in different scenarios. They work with developers, or the developers are prompt engineers, depending on how the company operates. Organizing their teams and prompt engineering makes AI applications more efficient and effective, because the response will be much more precise, right, and structured. Application developers typically encapsulate open-ended user input. Prompt engineer is one way to improve your output. In context learning is another way. In context learning means that your learning models may not know exactly or in detail about your application; however, your application and your request is not that complicated and simply is not known by the language model. Why not you tell the language model, "Oh, in my application, what I want is that if I have A, what I want to get is actually B," so A and B can be connected over the language model using your in-context learning to understand, "Oh, that's what you think about the relationship between A and B." You can tell the land model easily for that model to learn; that is called in-context learning.
There are a lot of words included here. Let me give you an example. I need to give you some terms to explain some terms and a few short learnings. Did you hear of few-shot learning before, or you may have heard n-shot learning? That means that n-way k-shot learning. Few-shot learning is a term you have to understand, because in general AI this is a very important, effective, and simple way to improve the results. N is the number of new categories or classes the model has to learn, and K is the number of labeled examples provided by each target category. In a lot of cases, when we use these language to explain our terms, it doesn't mean anything, right? It's very hard to understand.
Now let's look at this example. When n equals 3 and K equals 1, it means that we have three kinds of categories:
- positive
- neutral
- inactive
For each category, we give one example. That's why n equals 3 and K equals 1.
Now we tell the learning model:
- The food was wonderful, which means that it's positive.
- The service was okay, which means it's neutral.
- The atmosphere was terrible, which means it's negative.
For sentiment questions or problems, this is very good; these are very good, simple questions or simple statements for our language models to learn. Of course, this is too simple, even though we don't need or have language models. We can understand if we say "The atmosphere of a restaurant is terrible" is definitely the statement is negative, right, but in some cases there isn't an absolute value or answer for some questions. When something happens, is it good or not? Depending on the domains, right, so it's not our daily life, so we don't know if that's good or not. In those cases, you want to provide more information.
Another example over here is that n equals 3 but K is 2. For every category, we provide two examples:
- The food was fine, I love the restaurant (this is positive).
- The service was ok, it was good enough (this is neutral).
This one is good; neutral, I would say it's positive, right? You see, it depends on how you think about it, how you understand it. If you teach your language model that "good enough" means neutral, everything is neutral, right, because you wanted to think it's neutral; you don't want that learning model to think it's good. The atmosphere was terrible and the worst place ever is very obvious, is negative. These are examples why it's called in-context learning. While you tell your learning models how to understand your understanding of your domain, you can provide these kinds of few-shot learning examples to help at the time to understand your use case. Can the few-shot learning be used in very complicated use cases? No.For very complicated use cases, what you will see in a second, you will need to develop your models. Let's hear.

Few-shot learning is the most common approach to in-context learning and will meet the needs of most common enterprise applications. It eliminates the need for large amounts of labeled training data and extensive human testing and feedback. Varying for K generally run between one and five examples.

Is this in-context learning just like prompt engineering? Yes, you can say it is kind of prompt engineering. You are adding that as a context, right? Yeah, with the user-specific stuff, right? In context learning, the overall prompt requirements are that it has to be very clear. It has to provide some context, like just how we saw this in the restaurant, right, and the constraints, if any boundary conditions apply to the desired operation, they should be included in a prompt. This is what I just said: if you have extensive English for the main specific complexity, you must follow a complex pattern, impractical and unreliable trying. If you want to use some in-context learning, like a few-shot or COT (I forgot the name) Chain of thought is another way you can teach your LLMs how they can decompose your problems. For example, if you say:
- I have three apples.
- I bought three apples.
- I gave Sanit one apple.
- I bought two more.
How many apples do I have now? I know that's a very simple question for you guys. If you cannot quickly resolve this issue, you should go back, right? When we tell LLMs, we need to tell them how to decompose it. I have three apples, first step. Second step, I give Sanit one, and now I have 3-2, 3-1 equals 2. This is the second step. The third step is that I bought two more apples, and the fourth step is that I used my apple I left to class with two more I bought because of the whole, and then I have five apples, 4 apples. This is an example of how you decompose your problem. You can tell LLM models to follow this model to think; it's a chain of thoughts, right? To think in this way.

Okay, prompt engineering and in-context learning: the strength is that prompt engineering does not require fine-tuning or retaining a pre-trained model. In-context learning allows private and proprietary enterprise information to be processed by Contella Lab, and prompt style and sophistication can be matched to use cases. The weakness is that in-context learning is not retained from session to session because it's only for your current. We lose your examples, and we may not. We can, of course, for certain users, depending on the use cases, add those in-context learning examples in our prompt engine, our prompts, permanently. Of course we can do that, right? Prompt engineering may be insufficient or specialized or complex.

- The left side that we added. We learned we can use CooCityL, but here it also mentions the user interface, which is the console. Of course, we can operate on our cluster using CLI and console. We should never forget SDK. On any Kubernetes provider, there are very rich APIs for programmers to be interactive with it.
- Then we have these four major components of the Kubernetes master node or control plane.
- Then we have two worker nodes.
On the worker node, we already know that we need to have Docker runtime or container runtime. We are going to deploy some pods with our containers. We have these two small things:
- Kubelet. Kubelet is a node agent. On every single node, there is a node agent which is called kubelet. It can register the node with the API server, using the node's hostname or specific logic for a cloud provider. The kubelet is responsible for managing the deployment of pods to Kubernetes nodes, so it works with the scheduler. In the last lecture, we learned the scheduler is the major component that schedules which pod should go to which node. Kubelet is on the node, working on the node as a node agent to place the pod onto that node.
- Kube proxy. Kube proxy is purely a network tool on every node. It is a network proxy that runs on each node in your company. Okay, so the command line KubeCTL transforms your command line entries into API calls to interact with different parts of the Kubernetes cluster. This part today, when I demo you the cluster, you will see this part is there is a version on GKE that is the control plane. It contains all the components, but we won't see those components on the GKE console. We should understand these components are included in the control plane, and there is a version of the control plane, and it can be automatically updated by Kubernetes itself.

You can also watch a demo of how to create a GKE cluster and create an access TKE cluster from Cloud Shell. That's what I'm going to demo today. After you get to the prompt of Cloud Shell, you can issue `kubectl` to see a list of pods in a cluster. We don't only see a list of pods; we see a lot of things using the `kubectl` command.

The way it works is that we issue a command from the Cloud Shell. `kubectl` is going to send an API call through HTTPS to the control plane. Control plane, like I said in the last lecture, is the collective name of all the master nodes. It doesn't mean a specific node; it's a collective name. Control plane has the API server, which is a major component or commander in chief. The API server is going to send your request to the corresponding API components. The corresponding component is going to provide the API responses through HTTPS and then send it back to the user. The workflow is quite straightforward.

Now let's quickly look at these commands:
- `get`
- `describe`
- `logs`
`get` represents listing; it lists the status of the pods. If you specify pods, the phases could be:
- Pending
- Running
- Succeeded
- Failed
- Unknown
- CrashLoopBackOff
Pending means it's still fetching the image from the repository, and running means the container is already running inside of a pod. That's the status we really want to see. Succeeded means that you terminated your pods successfully, and failed means, for whatever reason, your pod is not successfully started. Unknown is really something we really don't want to see, because we don't know the reason. The last one happens very often when you use a QA case. When you look at the status of pods, `CrashLoopBackOff` means that your pod was already started and it was running, but for some reason it crashed. In that case, the error message is like this. This is a very general error; it only means that your pod has run and then crashed. There are a lot of different reasons that you can get this error message. If you search for it and find that your pod is in this status, you can use `describe` the pod to get the details of this error message. There should be an error code. Using this error with the error code, you can google it and find all different kinds of reasons why the part has a problem.
It can take a lot of time debugging this. It can be very time-consuming, because sometimes even though you have that error code when you google it, the error, the reasons, are not very clear. We use the notebook to develop the code for how machine learning works, right? We also need data, so the changes could be from the data. The changes could also be from the code; we connect with the code, right? Both the change of the data and the change of the code should be able to trigger the CI/CD pipeline to work automatically. When we make a stateful application into stateless, we don't store the state locally on the machine. We store the state in a database. So, a job is kind of a workload. When you submit it, it has to finish. When it finishes, it never starts again. You get it?

For deployment, in most cases, it's a service. It runs 24/7. It has to run all the time, but for jobs, obviously they are done. They are done. Okay, major differences between deployments and jobs. The major difference is how they handle a pod that is terminated. A deployment is intended to be a service; it should be up and running, and so it will try to restart the pods it manages to match the desired number of replicas. A job is intended to execute and is successfully terminated. In a deployment, the default restart policy of your policy set will always, but in a job it's never, only when it's not finished yet that the part is going to be restarted, right, so that's the major difference.

One very common example is that for a deployment it is like a web server or database server; they are always running because they are services. When you back up your database, it is a job, right? You will not back up your database in a given case, right, so after it is done, it is done, so it is a job. In general, it is a batch job, okay?

Yeah, you know what, another cluster scaling. When we say cluster scaling, we actually mean the node, not the power, okay? When we say we are scaling the node, we are using g-cloud because we are scaling from outside the cluster. Node is at the cluster level, and there is a node pool that our nodes are in. When you create a node, your node is put into a default pool; there is a default pool. You can create your own pool and you can deploy; you can create your nodes and put them in different pools.

Why do we have different pools? Just for different characteristics of computation. Like when you go to the swimming pool, the kids' pool, the adult pool, right? The kids' pool is very shallow, so now you can create your own pools like GPU pools, high memory pools for your use cases or developer pools, tester pools, and then you can put different, for different pools, you only allow different kinds of virtual machines or nodes to be launched in those pools, because their configurations are different, right? You can restrict what AMIs different pools can use. Different machines can be deployed only to those pools, and then you can define what users can use what pools. This way developers can only use the machines in the developer pool, right? The concept is quite simple.

When you try to scale the number of your nodes, you use T-Cloud. You specify the node pool; you can skip this part; that means you are using the default pool, and the number of nodes is six. You see, you are resizing the current number, so it is a fixed number; it is not a range, right? Say if the initial number is three, now you are resizing it to six. If you resize the number of nodes is and no parts in it, you got it? The better way is that we set a range. We are going to learn in a second how to scale. We want the number of nodes in our cluster to be from zero to some number; it's not a fixed number. You can also do it over here on the console.
When your cluster shuts down a node, it's going to monitor if the node is being used by parts, actually by containers. If a node is empty, it's going to shut down the node. If you are shutting down the application, assuming these containers are running the same application, you now want to shut down this application. The node that is going to be shut down is because you are shutting down the pods. In that case, you have to be very careful, because Kubernetes will randomly choose the node to shut down. If you are shutting down your node, you won't run that, right? When that is going to randomly choose one and shut down the application of all the parts in the node and then shut down the node. You have to understand what's going on before you shut down a node, say manually.
If a new part is coming in, the new container will be launched into a part, so we need to put that part into some node, right? When we put that part into some node, it's straightforward that, in our YAML file for the deployment job or part or whatever the object is, you can specify the number of the memory and the number of CPUs, right? You need to calculate, and Kubernetes will calculate the current capacity of the node and the capacity needed for the part, and then put that part into a node. The capacity on the node has to be enough for the part to run, right? This is very straightforward.
If you have empty nodes, not empty actually, Kubernetes checks your nodes' capacity every 10 seconds. If it finds that on some node both the CPU capacity and the memory capacity are below 50%, it will try to move all the workload on this node to other nodes and shut down this node. This is for others here. If you simply keep six or five nodes running all the time, they won't shut down any node. If you set up auto-scaling, it will do this.
When you put your node in a node pool, a node pool can be very big. The maximum number of nodes in one pool is 15,000 nodes, and we know that in one node we can have 108,000 parts. You can see when they are tied together how many containers we can run. One pod can run multiple containers, so from this you can understand how many pods maximally you can have in one node pool, but you don't know how many containers, because one pod can run multiple containers. It depends on how much resource each container needs, and then you know how many containers you can have in that pod. That's the default quota. If your application is huge, you need to request the quota increase from GCD.
When we use GCD mount, when we do the node autoscaling, not a class, okay? Node autoscaling, I said that we need to use GCD mount because we are doing it from outside the cluster. These are the commands. You don't need to memorize them:
- Create a cluster with autoscaling enabled.
- See, we just type this.
- Enable autoscaling for an existing node pool.
- We already have this node pool, and then we can enable autoscaling, minimum node and maximum node, and then we can add a node pool with autoscaling enabled.
- Disable autoscaling for an existing node pool.

Persistent data storage means what? Means your hard drives, right? The storage is very important, because in Kubernetes we are using nodes, which are virtual machines. On virtual machines, in a lot of cases, we are able to attach hard drives to that, right? In this context of GKE, we often say those hard drives with a general word; we should not say hard drives. The storage that we can use on GKE is called a volume. Volume is a more general word. We are going to see different volumes in a second.
There are two different kinds of volumes:
- Volumes can be persistent. When it is persistent, it represents hard drives or disk volumes. That's easy to understand.
- There is another volume; it's a directory which is accessible to all of the containers in the part, and some volumes are ephemeral and some are persistent. This one represents this one.
Ephemeral is one that is not that easy to understand. Temporary? Yeah, temporary. Let me explain. EmptyDir.
In this example, we are always talking about ephemeral and persistent in the context of a pod. For short-lived volumes, it means that you can mount that storage onto this pod. When this pod is gone, that volume is gone, that data is gone. That's why it's short-lived or ephemeral, right? EmptyDir is a concept in GKE.
Your pod is going to be launched inside one node, right? A virtual machine. A virtual machine has built-in hardware. Previously, when we learned AWS, we know that EC2 also has built-in hardware. The built-in hardware has a space which is instance store. That is ephemeral, right? Now, same thing on a GKE cluster. In the node, we also have built-in hardware. The built-in hard drive can be mounted onto your pod as /blah blah blah your directory name, but the actual storage is on the hardware of the node. That kind of disk space is called emptyDir. When the pod is gone, the corresponding storage on the hard drive is gone; the data is gone, so it's only good for using for caching or temporary data.
A persistent volume, we know, is as long as a hard drive. There are a few different kinds of ephemeral storage on GKE. Don't look at this one; it's out of our scope. If you remove the PVC, the corresponding PD is going to be removed at the same time, so your data is gone. Okay, but we have a way to keep it.
Now let's first understand the access mode. For any PDs, when you access it, you need to specify:
- read-write once
- read-only
- read-write many
Read-write once means that you can only read and, for the same node, you can read and write. It means that, in the same node, we are defining the access mode at a node level. Read-only means that this PD can be read from different nodes. Read-write many won't happen; you cannot read or write a PV from multiple nodes; that won't happen. This is not right. On EC2, it's the same thing. On AWS EC2, read-write many is not supported, although it says read-write many. In most cases, we use the first one, read-write once. Every PV can only be read by and written on the same node and cannot be accessed from other nodes. The same as EC2 is that. The same PV can only be attached to a node that is in the same region. Both on AWS and GCP, the hard drive and the virtual machine should be in the same region.

So for your PVC, if you want to prevent the corresponding PV to be removed when you remove the PVC object, you can put this retain policy in the PVC object definition. So, so you see this machine over here with gray and stuff. That's a node, an instance is a node. Let's say you're running Kubernetes locally and you want to connect multiple machines, multiple nodes. Your laptop is going to be one node. Be clear about that.
With Docker, you're able to run a single instance of an application using the `docker run` command, so you do `docker run` and it boots up a container on that node. With Kubernetes using the `kube control` you can run a thousand instances of the same application with a single command, so you say `kubectl` which is `kube control` run `replicas` thousand of my web server. This is a cluster of multiple nodes, so when we talk about nodes on cloud, it's basically multiple EC2 instances. We're running 1000 instances of the same application over 3 instances, and Kubernetes has control over all of them. Link will be rejoined. Okay, easier!
Now with the same Kubernetes, let's say you want to scale up to 2000. You can run the command `kube control scale` with the number of replicas you want for the existing application instances, and that 1000 will become 2000 over your whole cluster. You can also configure to do it automatically, but you also have control to do it manually whenever you want.
Now let's say you want to upgrade all these 2000 instances one at a time. You can run that with a single command called `rolling update`, so each instance will update and replace the old instance. You don't want everything to run in one go and everything to fade away. So let's say one instance runs if there's a fail, it's going to roll back into the old one, but let's say it's a successful one, it replaces the container that's been running with the old one. That's what rolling update is! It does one at a time, so you can see one by one its updating. If something goes wrong, it can help you roll back these images with a single command.
With Kubernetes, you're able to define the expected state of your application. Let's say your app has four different services:
- Your web server is running on 3 instances
- Your payment service (the purple thing over here) is on 2 instances
- Your Redis is running on 3 instances
- All like web server and payment servers are talking to this database or using this database
So, how would you define this in Kubernetes orchestration? You have a YAML file for each of these, so we're going to look at, we're going to break down the YAML file and talk about each of the fields. But on a high-level, since you have four different things, you will have four different YAML files, so you can maintain each of them separately.
This is for your pay server, you're saying, "I want two replicas for that." This is for your MySQL server, there's only one for that. Your Redis is three, and web server is also three.
So, right like I told you right now, a node is a machine, physical or virtual doesn't matter. It's a node, it's a machine where Kubernetes is installed. So, the clear thing is, a node is a worker machine, and this is where containers will be launched by Kubernetes. Right! But let's say what if the node where the app is running fails? The app goes down, there you don't have any fallback or you don't have any replicas or anything. So, let's say this fails, so we don't have any fallback. Hence, we always have a cluster of nodes. A cluster is a set of nodes grouped together. Even if one node fails, you have your application still accessible from the other nodes. Moreover, having multiple nodes helps in sharing load as well. So, having multiple replicas, there's two users for that. You can have availability, which is fault tolerance or resilience. Let's say this unusual amount of load tracks all your servers, you'll have more servers running. But let's say there's no error, but you still have a lot of users. You can load balance it and distribute it. So, there's two use cases for having a cluster of nodes.
Now, we have a cluster, right! But who is manageable? I mean, who is responsible for managing the cluster? Where is the information about the members of the class destroyed? How are the nodes monitored? If a node fails, how do you move the workload of the failed node to another worker node? All of these have to be taken care of. You can't just have a cluster of nodes with no master to control over it. That's where the control plane comes into place.
So, typically, when you're talking about a cluster, you'll have your number of worker nodes, but you'll also have an instance that is the control plane. So, when you think cluster of three nodes, implicitly, there's another node that's the control plane. Let's say you're using Okay, I forgot to tell you this. You won't have a node; all you'll have is the worker node. But if you're talking about using Kubernetes on cloud control plane, you don't have to manage at all!
So, you're trying to simulate multiple nodes, but not exactly node-to-node. It has a tiny, tiny node in your system itself, like VM. Ideally, yes, we can see how we can get all these nodes together. I mean, no wait, no workers. Technically, they can be anywhere, as long as the control plane has access to it. But usually, you want to keep it in the same region or you want to duplicate this whole cluster in another region. You wouldn't want to have one node here, one node here, three nodes in this region, three nodes in this region. So, control plane is another node with Kubernetes components installed in it. What it does is just watch over the nodes, the cluster, and is responsible for the actual orchestration of containers on the worker nodes.
So, in each of these, like control plane, worker, do you have specific components responsible for something in the orchestration? We're going to look at each of it. The control plane has four different components:
- Kube API server
- Etcd
- Controller manager
- Kube scheduler
Your worker nodes have three things:
- Kubelet
- Kube proxy
- Container runtime
Now, what is a kube API server? This is the core component server that exposes the Kubernetes HTTP API. So, when you're using CLI or UI to have control over what your orchestration is doing, you'll use this kube API server. It acts as the front-end for Kubernetes, so all the requests coming in go through kube API server. The users, management devices, CLI, all talk to the API server to interact with the cluster.
Control panel, yes, master! Basically, all the commands you do go through this component in the control plane. Because node will be directly called, no, you're making it front-facing! We're talking about a web server that's front-facing, so you're exposing a port to the outside thing, and then you can access it using IPN port. Normally, that's how we do it. AWS, no, no, this is only for managing your cluster and how the nodes talk to each other. How many nodes you want, how many containers you want running, all that. If you're saying on a worker node, I have a content running web server, if you expose a port out, you can directly access it if you don't have to access through API.
So, etcd, keystore, etcd is a distributed, reliable key-value store used by Kubernetes to store all data used to manage the cluster. It's consistent and highly available, so this is where the information about the nodes, the application running on the cluster, everything is stored. It's like a key-value store, so let's say the worker node sends a probe that says, "I'm healthy, I'm healthy, every two minutes," all that details is going to be stored in the etcd, so that other components can use it to make decisions accordingly. Yes, like its own database.
What is a controller manager? This runs controllers to implement Kubernetes API behavior, so it's basically the brain behind orchestration. They're responsible for noticing and responding when nodes, containers, or endpoints go down. So, let's say this node hasn't sent a probe in five minutes, then controller manager has checked etcd, updates that, and what it does is basically puts another worker node or puts does a restart or whatever that's what this handles. It makes sure the cluster is always healthy. These are the ones that make decisions to bring up new containers in such cases.
What is a kube scheduler? This is responsible for distributing the work of containers across multiple nodes, looks for parts not yet bound to a node, we look at pods in a minute, and assigns each pod to a suitable node. So, basically, these two work in hand, so both of them are responsible for the orchestration. The kube scheduler is responsible for moving the actual work, but the controller manager decides what work has to be done.
Just like that! Now, let's see how this works. A kubelet is an agent that runs on each node in the cluster. This agent is responsible for making sure the pods and containers are running on the nodes as expected. So, this is like a monitoring agent in each worker node, so this is what sends the probes and stuff like that. So let's say think of kube proxy as security groups. So kube proxy makes some rules, like "let's say this pod or this container can't talk to this container, but these containers can talk to each other." All those rules are like a network ACL.

Yes, kubelet is the approach and everything to the control plane. Container runtime is basically Docker. You want some container service to run within each worker node so it can run right! That's basically it. So it used to be Docker, but so kubelet was made for Docker initially in the early days. But then now Docker is not the only container service, so they've made it as a more general container runtime component of each worker node. It's called containerd. We don't usually say Docker now, but it's responsible for running containers because essentially containerd is running is just pulling an image from the Docker Hub or a container registry. So this has to always be there. You need to install a runtime into each node in the cluster so that pods can run there. We see the pods, pod is in a container creating state, and then soon changes to a running state when it is actually running.

So, talking about pod creation, let's talk about imperative vs declarative. So, what we saw right now, where we ran kubectl run NGINX, that is the most imperative way, so you just want a pod running with no specification, you just want it running. So, when you use commands like kubectl run or kubectl expose or kubectl scale, you're not specifying any YAML file with just specifications, right? You just want a quick ad hoc way to get the pods running, that's imperative.

But, usually, what I suggest you do is always declarative. Same thing as Terraform, you always have the code in a file, so you can revert back, and run it all at once. Same thing with this. Why would you want to launch a pod that has no control over, and then let's say you're scaling it later, but you have no control over what it has been before? Right? So, you don't have control over that.

So, always have a YAML file, declarative, better for version control and repeatability. Define objects in YAML manifest, and apply with kubectl apply -f. So, this is what it looks like. You can use create or apply. So, let's say you're modifying your YAML file, you'd use apply, but you can use create as well.

Let's talk about the components of the YAML file. Okay, so, the pod definition.yaml, I mean, you know how YAMLs are, you have children and sibling variables or fields. So, apiVersion, kind, metadata, and spec are all children, and in metadata, you have, I mean, not children, sorry, they're siblings. So, these four siblings, and in metadata, you have these children. So, you always have only these four fields: apiVersion, kind, metadata, and spec.

So, kind you can have four different options for this: you can have pod, we can look at these more, replica set, deployment, or service. You can have only four kinds. And then, metadata, usually put stuff like name and labels. And then, spec, inspect you specify the containers you're going to run in the pod, right? So, let's say for this part, for my app pod, which is the name, I want to run container with the name engine container, and with the image engine. So, let's say you have your own image on a Docker Hub repo, so how that would look like is let me just quickly show. So, I have this Docker name with an image name, that's it, that's how it looks like. Easier, easier length, just put that in, so we're done with pods now.

Let's look at replica set. So, all of these come under core objects, right? What is the replica set? It ensures a specified number of pod replicas run. Always. The replica set helps us run multiple instances of a single pod in the Kubernetes cluster, thus providing high availability. So, instead of having multiple pod YAML files, with one replica set YAML file, you specify how many replicas you want, let's say four, then, as soon as you run it, four pods are up and running, and they're all covered in this replica set. Yes, or one, yes. So, if the application crashes and the pod fails, users will no longer be able to access their application. So, this is before replica set to prevent users from losing access to our app, they would like to have more than one instance or pod running at the same time. That way, if one fails, we still have our application running on the other one. So, the replica set brings the failed one back to ensure a predefined number of replicas are always running. So, let's say you have four replicas running, and then we chose to delete one part, that's it, for a, b, c, and d are running, and we need parts e instantly, pod e is going to be deployed, because we said replicas 4. Main core object for these services, this is the most important. This is what networking comes in! You want a stable network endpoint in front of parts. What a service does is it enables connectivity between apps within the cluster as well as to expose apps outside the cluster to end users. Think of a service as a proxy or a load balancer, although it's technically not in a traditional sense. It provides an endpoint for other services to connect to.

So we have web server and database. How are these two talking to each other? We didn't talk about that. Let's talk about it now.

Classic example: you have web server container and a part. You have a Redis DB in a part, but you're saying a part each part has an IP also. So you have 10.244.0.2, 10.244.0.11 for these two parts. Now this pink arrow you see that is service.

Let's talk about it. So let's say we want this part to talk to this part. We can just start using IPs, right? Why shouldn't you use IP? Not safe, let's say this part goes down and a new part comes in which is rollback correct? Does it have the same IP? New port with new metadata, new IP. So you can't have that, right? I mean, it can, but that's what it shouldn't be because the IP for each part and it is bound to change at the port for to crash or restart for some reason. That's where a service comes in.

So what we do is we make a service called Redis DB, right? And we attach port service port to this spot port. On the virtual machines that you run Kubernetes on, you can very easily attach and mount object storage S3, although it doesn't happen very often. You need to install a package to mount S3, but the performance of the mount of S3 onto EC2 is not very good. A better solution, if you have to mount your data onto your EC2 instance, is using EFS, the shared file system, but it's a little bit more expensive, right? In most cases, when we use Kubernetes, we either mount EFS to the block device that you install on the virtual machine, or we simply use the block device that you attach to the virtual machine in the Kubernetes cluster, okay, or you use both at the same time if you have the budget.

When they upgrade by themselves, that means that the vendor pushed a new version. When they push a new version to your phone, they need to choose some strategies. Did you learn, did you hear blue-green strategy? Blue-green, canary, etc., those are all application deployment strategies.

In Kubernetes, it has a built-in rolling update. Rolling update means that the old version and the new version stay at the same time, so we are going to experience a process where we have both the old and the new version. Eventually, we gradually replace the old version fully by the new version. That's rolling update. We can push new updates; we can also push back if we have problems. On Kubernetes, we can do all these. They have built-in rolling updates, and we can also implement blue-green and canary deployment, but it needs more effort because it's not built-in. Okay, first Kubernetes objects. What does that mean? It's not a concept that's easy to explain, but it's better to see it to understand it by examples. A Kubernetes object is defined as a persistent entity that represents the state of something running in a cluster: it's desired state and its current state. For example, if you want to deploy a job (a job is actually a containerized application), you define this job itself as an object, but it doesn't mean we can define whatever object we want to define there are existing objects defined by Kubernetes like we have job object, deployment object, pod object, etc. These are all the components of a Kubernetes cluster. We are going to see more examples in the next few minutes, and then it's easy to understand. For whatever object you define, there are the desired state and the current state. The desired state is defined by your YAML files (just like when we learned ASP on Monday). The desired number of virtual missions is three; that's what we defined right? So now if we want to run a containerized application on Kubernetes, we need to write all the required parameters in a YAML file. We will submit this YAML file to the Kubernetes cluster. This YAML file represents the object the Kubernetes cluster is going to run and monitor, so the YAML file defines our desired status of this object. When Kubernetes runs it, it is going to monitor the status of the object, compare it with the desired object, a desired status we defined, always compare the current status and the desired status to make it the same as the desired status. We're going to see one diagram in a second about this. I know it's not very easy to understand, but now it's very abstracted. The current state described by Kubernetes, desired state is described by us. These are Kubernetes workload objects. You can see, pod is an object, job is an object, even replica set is an object, deployment is an object. The ones I circled, these four are the major objects we have learned in this course.

Now let's understand the concept of pod. Okay, so we know our applications are going to be deployed on the virtual machine, we know that version very well now right? On the virtual machine, we have our container run time to support our containers to run. So on the major difference between the virtual machines in Kubernetes and virtual machines in ECS (whatever environment) is that Kubernetes has pods on virtual machines. What is a pod? A pod is actually simply defined by software; it's not a hardware. It's defined by software, and it's a wrapper around your container to make it run and interact with the virtual machine and the Kubernetes cluster to make it executable and keep running. So, a pod you see over here is on your virtual machine. Every single virtual machine can have up to 110 pods. You can define this if you don't define it, it uses 110. You can define how many, maybe 3, maybe 4, on one virtual machine. Your application will be running in pods, okay, not on virtual machine directly. Pods are on virtual machines, and there could be 110 pods on one virtual machine. These three definitions about pods often occur in exams or interviews to ask, "Explain pods."

So, pods are the basic building blocks of standard Kubernetes model. They are the smallest deployable Kubernetes object. A pod is an object. Now you can understand Kubernetes object, right?

So, pods are the smallest execution unit in Kubernetes. You get it?

Sometimes people may say, "Container is the smallest deployable unit on Kubernetes." Actually, when you do not run containerized applications, we don't deploy anything onto Kubernetes because your container and your images are in container registries, they are not on Kubernetes. We only deploy them on the pods of your virtual machines in your K8s cluster, right?

So, we do not say containers are the smallest executable or deployable unit of Kubernetes. Pod is the smallest and executable unit.

You can deploy a few containerized applications in one pod, like this example. This is one single pod. We can run a few containerized applications on it, and if you run multiple containers on one pod, you need to distinguish the port numbers, right?

And also, in one pod, all the containers will share the storage and also share the networking between the containers in one pod. They use the local host to communicate (127.0.0.1) to communicate, and they share the same underlying storage, it could be hardware.

So, now let me see, on one virtual machine, you have multiple pods. It could be 110 in each part. You can run multiple containers, each container has its part, right? In each part, these containers can communicate using their internal (local host) IP address to communicate, get it?

Okay. On top of the operating system, this is just an application. Is a board-like an application? You cannot say it is an application. It's a component of Kubernetes. You see, it's the smallest deployable Kubernetes object. It's a part of Kubernetes architecture.

So, you can have multiple pods. One virtual machine can have 110 pods, very many!

So, basically, what is the thing that you wanted to manage? Why is it the unit that you are going to manage in the end? The pods or just the containers inside the pods?

You both! Actually, Kubernetes manages the pod because your containerized application will be deployed to the pod. Your application is developed by developers, right? Kubernetes doesn't care how you develop your application or what application you're going to deploy. They only deploy to the pod. If the pod has problems, it's going to restart or launch new pods. The problem of the pods could be caused by your application.

So, do you have like multiple one application running on multiple pods? Yeah, like different parts of the application running on different pods depending on the area of the application. You can, but different parts of your application running on different pods is possible if you have a good, you compose your application very well. And make sure every part can communicate very well on different pods. That works.

For example, you have like an application for a bank, maybe. You have a particular area that they share, like a storage, their network and resources. The managers will interact with API server frequently to send API calls and commands.
3. Scheduler (the component that will decide to place which pod onto which node). We have these as our worker node. These are virtual machines. Every single node is a virtual machine. So, scheduler controls to put a pod to which node. It's depending on the current status of the Kubernetes cluster and what you define in your YAML files to put your node, put your pod onto the node.
4. AppCV (a database). It stores all the metadata (all the information about the cluster, about your requirements and the status etc). All the data is stored over here. In order to learn load balancing services, you can see that AWS and CloudWatch are simple tools, but they are very important tools. Auto-scaling, of course, is a very important topic, but for this course it's all introductions to them.
Our objectives include:
1. We are going to indicate how to distribute traffic across EC2 instances by using load balancing. We call it elastic load balancing.
2. Later, we'll explain three major load managers.
3. Identify how CloudWatch enables you to monitor AWS resources and applications in real time.
4. How EC2 auto-scaling launches and releases servers in response to workload changes and performs scaling and load balancing tasks to improve an architecture.
First, let's look at elastic load balancing.It's a general term. It's not a specific service. It means that we can launch load balancers, and these load balancers are elastic. That means they can scale by themselves. Distribution of application or network traffic across multiple targets in a single AZ or across multiple AZs is a very important concept.
Load balancers are regional services. That means they can cover all the AZs. Even if you deploy a load balancer, it covers all the AZs in the same region. It doesn't go to other regions, okay?
The scale of your load balancer changes as traffic to the application changes over time. You don't need to worry about the scaling of load balancers, and you don't need to worry about this crash of your load balancer, because there will be a new one that will be scaled.
Previously, some student asked me this question. I deployed a load balancer in front of my application running on EC2 instances. Now, if my load balancer crashes, because load balancer is a managed service, when we use managed services, you cannot do much about it, right? If the load balancer crashes, that means we have a single point of failure. That's what we try to avoid, single point of failure, right?
When we learn that people in London, we are going to mention this again. There are a few architecture design principles or best practices. Single point of failure is one thing we need to try to avoid. If a load balancer crashes, that means people cannot access your EC2 instances, and your application runs on your EC2 instances, right? It's a single point of failure, right? AWS would not let this happen. They are going to replace the bad load balancer with a good load balancer, but you don't need to worry about that.
Generally, what it looks like is here: these are the clients that access applications using a load balancer. The applications could be running on EC2 instances or in containers. We could use load balancers to point to IP addresses and also to lambda functions. We're going to see the details on the next slide.
We have three kinds of load balancers:
1. ALB (Application Load Balancer)
2. NLB (Network Load Balancer)
3. CLB (Classic Load Balancer)
ALB represents you in load balancing HTTP or HTTPS traffic, and it's very good for modern application architectures, for example microservices and containers. When we make API calls, in a lot of cases we are making HTTP or HTTPS calls. In those cases, if your application is being called using HTTP or HTTPS, you can use an ALB in front of your application.
Another very important concept of ALB is that it runs at OSI layer 7, application level. This is the most important point for ALB.
Now, for NLB (Network Load Balancer), it is balancing TCP, UDP, and TLS traffic where extreme performance is required. That means when you use NLB, the performance is way better than an ALB, because you are at OSI model layer 4, the TCP transport layer, at the network transport layer. The network performance is way better than the application layer, which is level 7, the application layer, which is on top of your TCP layer. That's why, when you see questions in the future in exams, when people need super high performance and low latency, what kind of load balancer is it, definitely NLB. NLB can handle interrupt traffic to targets based on IP protocol data, and it can handle millions of requests per second and maintain ultra low latencies. HTTP and TCP we are more familiar with, because we did a lot of client-server application development. What kind of application would use TCP or UDP?ULPs for real-time data and PCP, I think, for web software also. Give me some example applications. Like, we are getting the nice data from software saying, "That's a UDP user. You're reading databases." They call it WebSockr. WebSockr and database. WebSockr is a PCB, right? What? WebSockr is a PCB? Yeah. We are talking about TCP/UDP or TLS. All of them, but these all fall into the N. We can use NLB. We will read the databases. It's definitely not HTTP, right? It's TCP. I saw you said when you play games, you use SSH? No. Okay, so you can see when you play games, the latency is very, very small, right? Very low, because it uses TCP, and so that's why, when we design our applications, we may have, say, this is our database instances. We may have many of them. If in front of databases we want to have a load balancer, we can have an NLB to make it very quick and balance the traffic. When we say we use a load balancer, we are just using the traffic to different instances, right? Depending on the traffic, how easy it is, and which instance is healthy. When we have a user interface, if we want to put something in front of user interface load balancer in front of user interface instances, then it should be at ALB, right? You can use client-server HTTP proxy, right? In a bigger application architecture, there could be public-facing load balancers and there could be internal load balancers. For example, this NLB, it can never be a public-facing load balancer, right? It has to be internal, because it's in front of database instances. For this one, ALB before the user interfaces is a public-facing load balancer. It supports all the particles, and you see it offers both application and transport layer, but we don't use them. I won't tell you more about it, although this course is not that; we should not discuss that much. I want to let you know what good things you can do using load balancers. We used to mention that, when you have user interfaces, if your user interfaces are running, say, on EC2 instances, you put those EC2 instances in a public subnet, right, and then connect to the outside using an Internet Gateway. Your instances are all public-facing; people can access them directly from the internet. Now I want to protect my user interface instances. How do I do it? I can put all the user interface instances, the user interface front end servers, in a private subnet. I put an ALB in a public subnet. The ALB will get the request from users from the internet and direct the request through the ALB to the user interface instances. This is a recommended way, so you can keep all your instances in so when people access your ALB in order to access your front-end, which is in a private subnet, you can assign the ALB a DNS name. You can give the ALB a DNS name, and this DNS name will have an alias. The alias is the DNS name of those instances in the private subnet. This way, all the subnets, all the instances, front-end instances in the private subnet share the same unique DNS name. Your users only access that DNS name, but their traffic will be distributed to different instances in the private subnet. You get it? This is a very good way to protect your instances and keep all your instances, even the public-facing instances, in private subnets. Okay? Can CloudFront usually is at the very front, before ALB, because CloudFront is kind of cache, right? What do we put in front of ALB? We're taking more than we use today. Now, what do you do? Now you can set up a well-53 DNS because Route 53 is a global service. Route 53 can route user requests to either LV-A or LV-B. You get it? That way, your user request can be routed to two regions. Okay. Load balancer compared to ALB and network load balancer compared to ALB, it has one more advantage: it can be attached to an elastic public IP address. In that way, NLB can directly connect to some resources, even on-premise. If you have an on-premise server which has a public IP address, you can use NLB to directly connect with that on-premise server, but ALB cannot. ALB can only connect to those instances in your VPC or even subnets. NLB actually is quite powerful. This is a brief introduction to them. Now let's look at how it works, still at a very high level. When you use them, you register targets in target groups. The targets are the instances you are trying to use behind the load balancers. They are registered as target groups and route targets to the target groups. With classic load balancers, you register instances with the load balancer. You don't register target groups, so we only explain the target groups. In this example, you can see we have AZ-A and AZ-B, and we have a load balancer in AZ-A and a load balancer in AZ-B. You may think, "Why do I have two load balancers?" When you try to provision load balancers in a region, you need to tell which AZs you are going to deploy your load balancers. If you choose two AZs or three AZs, depending on how many AZs you choose, the number of load balancers will be the same as the number of AZs you choose. For each AZ, there will be a load balancer deployed for you. All the load balancers share the same DNS name. If load balancer A and B don't share the same DNS name, your users request when they are coming into your instances or targets through different load balancers in different AZs, how can they know they are using the same load balancer for the same application server? Right? Load balancer A and B in AZ-A and AZ-B share the same DNS name, but they are provisioned in different AZs for the instances in the AZ. You got it? Okay, so now you have a load balancer in AZ-A and a load balancer in AZ-B. They listen to the request, check for connection request, and they will distribute your request to the instances. Actually, your tactic is you register your instances as targets, so it only routes the traffic to healthy targets. That means it checks the health of the targets like this one. If it doesn't work, it crashes; it won't route traffic to that target. Actually, the load balancer should. If it detects an unhealthy instance, it should remove this target from this load balancer. If a new instance is launched, it should register to the load balancer. That's why you don't need a manager. Okay, later we'll learn ASG and Auto Scaling Rule, right. When it automatically scales, you either scale out or scale in. When you scale out, you will need to register more instances in the same load balancer. That's not done by you; that's done by the load balancer. When you scale in, some instances will be removed, so the load balancer will remove their registry in the load balancer. This is how it works. Elastic load balancing use cases are high availability and fault-tolerant applications. Now let's look at CloudWatch. Still at a very high level, again. Like I said, CloudWatch should be used in almost any application. You will see them. What it can help you with is: how do you know when you should launch more EC2 instances? Basically, the load is full; we need to get more instances. The main topic for today, one of the main topics, is autoscaling. We need to use autoscaling groups (ASG) to set up the autoscaling instance group and set up the policies when we are going to scale out and scale in to make it dynamic. When you set up an ASG, you need to specify the minimum size of this group, say two instances, and the maximum size of this group, say four instances. You also specify the desired capacity, which is a very interesting concept. I'm going to give you some explanation about that.

An autoscaling group is a collection of easy instances that are treated as a logical grouping for the purposes of automatic scaling and management. When we scale out and scale in, this is the base configuration. When you are at your base configuration, when you have two instances, if there is nothing, there is no request to come in; that is the base configuration. When we are at a base configuration, that means we have the number of desired instances. This is the desired instance, which is two, and then we can, when we scale out, depending on our policy in the future where I'm going to learn policies, we need to add one more instance into the upscale group. Later, we will need to scale in; we terminate one instance. We got two instances, so it's easy to understand scale out and scale in.

What do we do in order to get an autoscaling group set up? We need to specify where we are going to put the autoscaling group. You need to specify the VPC and the subnet, and then the group is going to be in that subnet. You also need to tell ASG what you are going to put in the subnet. We learned that before, right? AMI; we need to construct an AMI and store that AMI. It's called my AMIs, right? Our AMIs, so you store them. On the launch configuration on the console, you specify all these, so all the future EC2 instances will be launched using the configuration you specified. You specified a load balancer before the group, so now maintain current number actually means maintain the desired number.

- Scheduled scaling

- Predictive scaling

A typical pipeline for end-to-end integration processing would look something like this:
- You have local instances or EC2 instances or IoT sensors pushing data to KDS. You can think of that as a decoupler, like SQS; it's like a huge queue.
- You have multiple consumers getting from it.
- Glue is a service which we're going to talk about today. That is for ETL (extract, transform, and load), so that can fetch the data from the KDS and then push to S3.
- You can look at Amazon Athena, which is basically querying data lake objects. Think of that like SQL but for objects in S3.
- In the end, you can have Amazon QuickSight, which is like a BI tool. You can have an analytics dashboard, all kinds of pie charts, everything can be done using this service.
End-to-end processing on AWS with something like this, we're talking about streaming plus analytics. This processing can allow for real-time ingestion, transformation, querying, and visualization. We have four services for that:
- Amazon KDS
- Glue
- Athena
- QuickSight
Let's do a recap of KDS. It is a managed streaming ingestion for high-throughput and low-latency data. What happens is that latency is high because you're doing in batches. You are ingesting batches of data and then crossing it once in a go, but this one's more near real time because, as soon as there's a new event, you're trying to process that data. That data is retained for a configurable window (hours, today can be replayed). I think the default was 24 hours, and then you can extend that up to one year. You can also have multiple consumers polling into the KDS. Here, you can have Lambda, analysis, analytics, customer apps, or Blue Jobs. Producers are apps or IoT devices, logs, click streams, and push events to KDS, and then KDS buffers and distributes these events to downstream processors and the downstream processor.
We ingest using Kinesis data streams. We use S3 for storage because we want a data lake. It's not processed yet; we just want the unprocessed data to be stored somewhere for now. We can store the raw and curated zones. What is curated is when we work on AWS Glue.
