So how does a typical work look like in AWS Glue?
1. You define your data sources and targets in the data catalog
2. You use crawlers to populate data catalog with table metadata from the data sources
3. Data catalog is with schema tables source is your S3 in this case
4. You define ETL jobs with transformation scripts to move and process data
5. You can run jobs on demand or based on triggers
6. You can monitor job performance using dashboards
So what are transformations? They're just like common, think of like SQL operations like join, aggregate, filter or apply mapping so apply mapping would rename and re-order columns, filter would remove unwanted rows based on conditions, join combines data from multiple sources and aggregate groups and calculate summaries. That's it with AWS Glue. Any questions with respect to Glue?So let's go to Athena. So like I said before think of Athena as SQL for S3. It's just an interactive query service that simplifies data analysis in S3 using standard SQL. Okay, so in this section, we learned that AWS and customer share security responsibility. AWS is responsible for the security of the cloud, and we are responsible for the security in the cloud. AWS is responsible for protecting the infrastructure, and for services that are categorized as infrastructure-as-service, the customer is responsible for performing necessary security competition and management tasks.

Now let's look at IAM, which is a very important thing about cloud, but we don't have a lot of time learning this. IAM is the name. The full name is Identity and Access Management. So if you are a cloud engineer or managing your cloud infrastructure or platform for your organization, IAM will be the thing you will need to use almost every day. Examples of how IAM roles are used to delegate access:
- Used by IAM users in the same AWS account as the role
- Used by an AWS service such as EC2 in the same account as the role
- Used by an IAM user in a different AWS account than the role
In the same account is easier to understand. EC2 instance can access the S3 package, we assign a role to the EC2 instance. Block Devices or Block Store is the hard disk we talk about in our daily life. Okay?

Block devices, we are so familiar with it. When we use hard drives, we can load the file and then we can modify the file, right? Then we can save the file. You are working on the same file. You are modifying part of the file, is that right? When you use hard drives, when you load a file, edit it, you are modifying part of the file. That is called the block storage.

However, in the context of Cloud, there's a very important concept of storage, which is called object storage. The files you use on block storage can be uploaded to an object storage and viewed as an object. Every single object is actually also a file. The major difference here is that, for block storage, you can change one block, like here, or, like I said, you can change part of the file; however, for object storage, the entire file must be updated. Say if you have file one already uploaded to object storage, now you want to change it. You cannot load object one, change it, and save it back. No, that doesn't work. You have to upload a new object. You can modify it, and then you have to upload a brand new object to the storage with the same name to replace the previous object. Okay, so that's a major difference between them.

Now I have a question for you: Are files in object storage immutable? Who can answer me? Yes, because you cannot change part of the file. What storage should I use if I want to modify part of the file? This is a major difference between object storage and block storage. Now let's look at some features of EDS. In the future I won't say "elastic blocks" or "I just said EDS, okay?" Don't misunderstand it as being stocked.
The EDS enables you to create individual storage volumes in the context of the cloud. We often use the term "volumes." It's identical to disks. Whenever you hear "volumes," it means disks with some specified size (50 GB, 100 GB), and you attach them to an EC2 instance, just like when you, by permission, want to attach a USB drive.
You can create this disk virtually on the cloud and then attach it to an EC2 instance. It is block-level storage. Volumes are automatically replicated. This is very important. When you create one EVS volume, it is automatically replicated within its availability zone, so you have redundant data of your storage, but you never notice that because they don't explicitly tell you where your replications are. Those are only for failover.
I used to mention that, whenever we mention the resiliency of computing, the first word we need to think of is "failover," right? Cloud providers provide themselves with the ability to failover. When one of your drives fails, they can failover to another one, but you don't notice it; they keep your copies. It can be backed up automatically to S3 through snapshots. This is very important in real industry work. If your application or your workload is in production, you always need to have a backup. When you back up your hard drive, you always keep a snapshot.

Now let's look at S3, the simple storage service. You will see this picture very often in the picture, right? Whenever we see this, we know we are going to put things in it as S3. I don't know if you've heard of one term before, data lake. Data Lake sounds like a term that's not easy to understand. It's actually very simple. A data lake is a kind of storage solution to store your raw data. The best storage solution for a data lake is object storage on TCP on Azure. They all have their own object storage solutions, but those solutions in general are all object storage and are for raw data or for data as a data lake.

In the future, when you have a use case telling you, "Oh, we need a data lake," you immediately should think about object storage. This is very commonly happening in the context of data analytics, data engineering, and AI applications because we need raw data. You need raw data, then you need to process the data, then you need to analyze the data, and then you need to visualize the data. At the very beginning, it is all raw data. That is a data lake.

When we implement the data lake, we always use whatever way we have image data, audio data, words, spreadsheets; they are all files, but now what is an object? When we put a file in an object storage, this file is not a simple file; it has very rich metadata. This metadata is managed and contains even the data of the hardware where it is in AWS's data center. It contains a lot of metadata, and this single file can be represented using a URL so you can access this data by HTTP. It contains much more information than the previous normal file. That's why it's called an object.

Object storage is usually very, very big. We say it's unlimited. Of course, theoretically, nothing is unlimited, but why do we say object storage is unlimited? Because all the cloud providers are investing money endlessly to increase the size of their object storage, so you never feel you don't have enough space in object storage. That's why we say it's unlimited. Even one single file or one single object can be as big as 5 TB. Have you ever seen a file that is 5 TB? No. Yes? Okay, great. I have never seen a single file that is as big as 5 TB before. It's very rare, but you can see in the object storage that you can have that big file, and it's one file. It's designed for 11/9 of durability. 11/9 of durability means what? Nine-nines. Yes, not seven-nines, nine-nines. 99.99. Is that right? Yeah, because we totally have eleven nines, right? It means that you may lose one object in 10 million years. That means you won't lose your five, right, in ten million years. We don't even know in a hundred years or a 1000 years what happens, what it looks like, right, so that's why we say, using object storage, your data is 100% safe. All the policies for archived data are one write, many read. After the one write is done, nobody can write it again.
